<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">An 18-second sample of "All About That Bass", featuring Trainor singing the track's pre-chorus, which is complemented by a scatting tempo, shimmying melody and early 1960s soul-pop groove.</div>
</div>
<hr/></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>"All About That Bass" is a <a href="/wiki/Bubblegum_pop" title="Bubblegum pop">bubblegum pop</a>/<a href="/wiki/Doo-wop" title="Doo-wop">doo-wop</a> and retro-<a href="/wiki/Rhythm_and_blues" title="Rhythm and blues">R&amp;B</a> song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DS_review_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-DS_review-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slant_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-slant-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vox_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-vox-3">[3]</a></sup> Using the 4/4 <a href="/wiki/Time_signature" title="Time signature">time signature</a> with a tempo of 134 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beats_per_minute" title="Beats per minute">beats per minute</a>, the song's <a href="/wiki/Key_signature" title="Key signature">key signature</a> is in <a href="/wiki/A_major" title="A major">A major</a>; Trainor's vocals span the tonal nodes of <a href="/wiki/E_(musical_note)" title="E (musical note)">E<sub>3</sub></a> to <a href="/wiki/C%E2%99%AF_(musical_note)" title="C♯ (musical note)">C♯<sub>5</sub></a>. The song follows a basic <a href="/wiki/Chord_progression" title="Chord progression">chord progression</a> of A–Bm–E–A<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sheet_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-sheet-23">[23]</a></sup> invoking a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Throwback_style" title="Throwback style">throwback</a> musical style of the <a href="/wiki/1950s_in_music" title="1950s in music">1950s</a> and <a href="/wiki/1960s_in_music" title="1960s in music">1960s</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Out_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Out-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Idolator_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Idolator-25">[25]</a></sup> Critics recognize elements from a variety of genres: <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">hip hop</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Out_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Out-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Country_music" title="Country music">country</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">rock and roll</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Idolator_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Idolator-25">[25]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Earworm" title="Earworm">earworm</a> hook,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slate_2_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slate_2-26">[26]</a></sup> early 1960s <a href="/wiki/Soul_music" title="Soul music">soul-pop</a> <a href="/wiki/Groove_(music)" title="Groove (music)">groove</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USA_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-USA-27">[27]</a></sup> scatting <a href="/wiki/Tempo" title="Tempo">tempo</a> and shimmying <a href="/wiki/Melody" title="Melody">melody</a> are also present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slate_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slate-28">[28]</a></sup> Trainor delivers a hint of<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stereogum_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stereogum-29">[29]</a></sup> Caribbean <a href="/wiki/Reggae" title="Reggae">reggae</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beast_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Beast-20">[20]</a></sup> in addition to a variety of background vocal and rapping techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopMatters_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopMatters-30">[30]</a></sup> Wordless vocal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ad-lib" title="Ad-lib">ad-libs</a> in the song's outro feature Trainor pitching down an echoing "bass, bass, bass" at the end of the chorus mark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stereogum_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stereogum-29">[29]</a></sup> The chorus consists of Trainor repeating "I'm all about that bass, 'bout that bass, no treble".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beast_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Beast-20">[20]</a></sup> The vocals of "All About That Bass" have been compared to the harmonies of 1960s girl groups and vintage 1950s singers <a href="/wiki/Betty_Everett" title="Betty Everett">Betty Everett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doris_Day" title="Doris Day">Doris Day</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eydie_Gorm%C3%A9" title="Eydie Gormé">Eydie Gormé</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rosemary_Clooney" title="Rosemary Clooney">Rosemary Clooney</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slate_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Slate-28">[28]</a></sup> "All About That Bass" has also been described as melodically similar to the 2006 song "Happy Mode" by South Korean group <a href="/wiki/Koyote" title="Koyote">Koyote</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Channel_V_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Channel_V-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>For some, including the singer, the lyrics of "All About That Bass" provide a callout to embrace inner beauty,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> and to promote a positive <a href="/wiki/Body_image" title="Body image">body image</a> and self-acceptance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Chris_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Chris-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The words "treble" and "bass" in the song act as metaphors for the weight of women and serve as a joke about thick and thin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian_Interview_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_Interview-7">[7]</a></sup> The line "I'm bringing booty back" references <a href="/wiki/Justin_Timberlake" title="Justin Timberlake">Justin Timberlake</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/SexyBack" title="SexyBack">SexyBack</a>" (2006).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stereogum_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stereogum-29">[29]</a></sup> In the song, Trainor criticizes the fashion industry for creating unreachable standards of beauty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Today_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Today-37">[37]</a></sup> Its lyrical message has been compared to <a href="/wiki/Kesha" title="Kesha">Kesha</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/We_R_Who_We_R" title="We R Who We R">We R Who We R</a>" (2010), <a href="/wiki/Pink_(singer)" title="Pink (singer)">Pink</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Fuckin%27_Perfect" title="Fuckin' Perfect">Fuckin' Perfect</a>" (2010), <a href="/wiki/Sara_Bareilles" title="Sara Bareilles">Sara Bareilles</a>' "<a href="/wiki/Brave_(Sara_Bareilles_song)" title="Brave (Sara Bareilles song)">Brave</a>" (2013), and <a href="/wiki/Robin_Thicke" title="Robin Thicke">Robin Thicke</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Blurred_Lines" title="Blurred Lines">Blurred Lines</a>" (2013), among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Atlantic_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Atlantic-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vice_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vice-39">[39]</a></sup> The song was compared to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Mix-A-Lot" title="Sir Mix-A-Lot">Sir Mix-A-Lot</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Baby_Got_Back" title="Baby Got Back">Baby Got Back</a>" (1992), and other 2014 songs "<a href="/wiki/Anaconda_(Nicki_Minaj_song)" title="Anaconda (Nicki Minaj song)">Anaconda</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Nicki_Minaj" title="Nicki Minaj">Nicki Minaj</a> and "<a href="/wiki/Booty_(song)" title="Booty (song)">Booty</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Lopez" title="Jennifer Lopez">Jennifer Lopez</a> for its references to the female <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Derriere" title="Derriere">derriere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Booty_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Booty-40">[40]</a></sup> The song has also been described as a modern version of American singer <a href="/wiki/Christina_Aguilera" title="Christina Aguilera">Christina Aguilera</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Beautiful_(Christina_Aguilera_song)" title="Beautiful (Christina Aguilera song)">Beautiful</a>" (2002).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sullivan_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sullivan-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Release">Release</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Release">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>"All About That Bass" served as Trainor's debut single,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4Music_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-4Music-41">[41]</a></sup> and was released as the lead single from her 2014 debut <a href="/wiki/Extended_play" title="Extended play">extended play</a> (EP) <i><a href="/wiki/Title_(EP)" title="Title (EP)">Title</a></i> as well as her 2015 debut major-label <a href="/wiki/Title_(Meghan_Trainor_album)" title="Title (Meghan Trainor album)">studio album</a> by the same name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The single was first <a href="/wiki/Music_download" title="Music download">digitally</a> released worldwide on June 30, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aus1_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aus1-43">[43]</a></sup> Epic solicited the song to <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_hit_radio" title="Contemporary hit radio">contemporary hit radio</a> in the United States on July 1, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radio_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Radio-44">[44]</a></sup> Streaming and digital release dates were held back on different dates for the song to increase download sales in United Kingdom (UK).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Analysis_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Analysis-45">[45]</a></sup> The song was made available to <a href="/wiki/Streaming_media" title="Streaming media">stream</a> on <a href="/wiki/Spotify" title="Spotify">Spotify</a> in the UK on August 14, 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Analysis_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Analysis-45">[45]</a></sup> and made available to download on September 28, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UKdownload_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-UKdownload-46">[46]</a></sup> An "All About That Bass" EP was released in Austria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aus2_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aus2-47">[47]</a></sup> Germany, and Switzerland on October 3, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ger2_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ger2-48">[48]</a></sup> The EP featured the tracks "Title", "<a href="/wiki/Dear_Future_Husband" title="Dear Future Husband">Dear Future Husband</a>", and "Close Your Eyes" as its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B-side" title="B-side">b-sides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swi2_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swi2-49">[49]</a></sup> On the same day, a <a href="/wiki/CD_single" title="CD single">CD single</a> for the song was released in Germany, with "Title" as its b-side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CD_single_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-CD_single-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>An alternate version of "All About That Bass" was re-recorded by Trainor, with more family-friendly lyrics of her choice, for its impact date on <a href="/wiki/Radio_Disney" title="Radio Disney">Radio Disney</a> in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Disney_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Disney-51">[51]</a></sup> Trainor and Kadish agreed to the alternate version of "All About That Bass" on the condition that it be relegated to Radio Disney and not be made available for purchase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-10">[10]</a></sup> Radio Disney vice-president of programming, Phil Guerini, felt the alternate version ensured support from their targeted kids and family audience. The re-recording was later used on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adult_Contemporary" title="Adult Contemporary">Adult Contemporary</a> radio in the United States. Assistant program and music director of radio station <a href="/wiki/WHUD" title="WHUD">WHUD</a>, Tom Furci, stated he and program director Steve Petrone were reluctant to add the song on their radio playlist. However, Furci said they realized they could not refuse to play a national success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Disney_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Disney-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reception">Critical reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Critical reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A variety of critics tipped it as 2014's strong candidate for "Song of the Summer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Out_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Out-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Idolator_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Idolator-25">[25]</a></sup> It was later awarded the title in a poll held by NBC's <i>Today</i> in September 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a> writer Paul Grein deemed it "one of the biggest and best 'message' songs of recent times".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yahoo.21_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yahoo.21-53">[53]</a></sup> <i>The Guardian</i> journalist Caroline Sullivan stated that the song was significant on combating "the media-propagated notion" that only small buttocks are sexy and said the track had the "aura of a hard-won victory against self doubt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sullivan_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sullivan-16">[16]</a></sup> Evan Sawdey of <i><a href="/wiki/PopMatters" title="PopMatters">PopMatters</a></i> commended the track's lyrics for showcasing Trainor's personality and wrote that it was "one of the funnest songs" of 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopMatters_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-PopMatters-30">[30]</a></sup> Erik Ernst of the <i><a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_Journal_Sentinel" title="Milwaukee Journal Sentinel">Milwaukee Journal Sentinel</a></i> called it "universally catchy" and "shamelessly dance-ready".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Gary Trust of <i>Billboard</i> felt that the song sounded completely different from every other pop song on radio at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Interview_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Interview-55">[55]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> journalist Hugh McIntyre described it as "insanely catchy," "easy-to-love" and "heartwarming," adding that it would be an interesting song for young children listen to, while praising Trainor's lyrics choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beejoli Shah of <i>The Guardian</i> stated the song has "instantly-memorable" lyrics and "addictive" bass line, but opined that "All About That Bass" was a <a href="/wiki/Novelty_song" title="Novelty song">novelty song</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shah_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shah-57">[57]</a></sup> Jon Carmanica of <i>The New York Times</i> echoed Shah's view and called "All About That Bass" a "cheeky novelty hit," and quipped, "'I'm bringing booty back,' she sings, though there's probably already a line forming to assure her it never went anywhere".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmanica_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carmanica-58">[58]</a></sup> In a mixed review, <i>Slate</i> journalist Chris Molanphy commended the tempo and melody used in the lyric, "Every inch of you is perfect / From the bottom to the top," which he felt was "effortlessly memorable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slate_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Slate-28">[28]</a></sup> He went on to compliment Kadish's production on the song and felt that he created a "wispy tune in vintage white-girl, Italo-Latin soul, expertly aping the sound of peak <a href="/wiki/Rosemary_Clooney" title="Rosemary Clooney">Rosemary Clooney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eydie_Gorm%C3%A9" title="Eydie Gormé">Eydie Gormé</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slate_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-Slate-28">[28]</a></sup> However, Molanphy was critical of the track's use of the word "treble" as a metaphor and quipped, "Let's just hope Trainor's gross misuse of 'treble' as a metaphor doesn't wreck the word's definition for a generation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slate_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-Slate-28">[28]</a></sup> In a negative review, <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> named "All About That Bass" as the fifth worst song of 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognition_and_accolades">Recognition and accolades</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Recognition and accolades">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>"All About That Bass" garnered praise from a variety of Trainor's contemporaries, including Beyoncé,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Becky_G" title="Becky G">Becky G</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Becky_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Becky-61">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Colbie_Caillat" title="Colbie Caillat">Colbie Caillat</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Interview_2_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Interview_2-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Maejor" title="Maejor">Maejor Ali</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maejor_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maejor-62">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Miranda_Lambert" title="Miranda Lambert">Miranda Lambert</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMT_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-CMT-63">[63]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/T-Pain" title="T-Pain">T-Pain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EW_Interview_2_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-EW_Interview_2-14">[14]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> ranked the track fourth in their top songs of 2014 list,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> while <i><a href="/wiki/Cosmopolitan_(magazine)" title="Cosmopolitan (magazine)">Cosmopolitan</a></i> listed the track at #17 on their "Best Songs of 2014" publication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In the annual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pazz_and_Jop" title="Pazz and Jop">Pazz and Jop</a> mass critics poll of the year's best songs in 2014, "All About That Bass" was ranked at number 23.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The song was nominated in the category, Best Song with a Social Message, at the <a href="/wiki/2014_MTV_Europe_Music_Awards" title="2014 MTV Europe Music Awards">2014 MTV Europe Music Awards</a>, but lost to Beyoncé's "<a href="/wiki/Pretty_Hurts_(song)" title="Pretty Hurts (song)">Pretty Hurts</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> It was nominated in the category for Favorite Song at the <a href="/wiki/41st_People%27s_Choice_Awards" title="41st People's Choice Awards">41st People's Choice Awards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> For the <a href="/wiki/57th_Annual_Grammy_Awards" title="57th Annual Grammy Awards">57th Annual Grammy Awards</a>, the song was nominated for <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Record_of_the_Year" title="Grammy Award for Record of the Year">Record of the Year</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Song_of_the_Year" title="Grammy Award for Song of the Year">Song of the Year</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy">Controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The song's lyrics became a subject of controversy among music critics who felt that the song did not promote a positive body image as Trainor intended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Atlantic_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Atlantic-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vox_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vox-70">[70]</a></sup> Trainor was accused of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-feminism" title="Anti-feminism">anti-feminism</a> and shaming thin women in the song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vox_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vox-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-71">[71]</a></sup> Kris Ex of <i><a href="/wiki/Complex_(magazine)" title="Complex (magazine)">Complex</a></i> said that the track "jacked both the stereotyped body priorities of black beauty and the codes of black slang".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Complex_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Complex-5">[5]</a></sup> Naomi Schaefer Riley of the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Post" title="New York Post">New York Post</a></i> dubbed the song as "faux empowerment" and wrote, "feminists want her to know she's not going to get away with it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Kelsey McKinney of <a href="/wiki/Vox_Media" title="Vox Media">Vox</a> felt that some verses in "All About That Bass" uplifted ideas of body positivity and self-worth while destroying those ideals in others by putting down other women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vox_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vox-70">[70]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i> writer Yomi Adegoke said that the song's replacement of one ideal with another was a poor representation of body positivity, and felt that its enforcement that "real men love curves" was insulting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Independent_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Independent-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Trainor, who did not identify as a feminist at the time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feminism_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-Feminism-9">[9]</a></sup> responded, "I didn't work this hard to hate on skinny people, I wrote the song to help my body confidence—and to help others."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jim_Farber_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jim_Farber-13">[13]</a></sup> <i>PopMatters</i> writer Evan Sawdey defended the song, writing, "This song is so positive, so energetic, and so universal—it's something people of all shapes and sizes can get behind".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopMatters_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-PopMatters-30">[30]</a></sup> Caroline Sullivan of <i>The Guardian</i> wrote that the fact critics took offense to the song's lyrical content, manifested the delicacy of the issue of body image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sullivan_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sullivan-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>In an interview in 2016, Trainor claimed she's a feminist: "I’d been told: ‘Don’t say you’re something if you don’t know what it is.’ So I was like: ‘Well, I’m not a feminist,’ because I didn’t really understand it and then I was like ‘Oh shit, obviously I am a feminist. My songs are women’s anthems across the board."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="for something like this, surely there is a more reliable source than Bustle? (May 2016)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> .</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_performance">Commercial performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Commercial performance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>"All About That Bass" topped the national charts of 19 countries internationally and had sold over 11 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ifpi2015_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ifpi2015-75">[75]</a></sup> "All About That Bass" was a <a href="/wiki/Sleeper_hit" title="Sleeper hit">sleeper hit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rolling_Stone_2_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rolling_Stone_2-76">[76]</a></sup> In the United States, "All About That Bass" debuted at number 84 on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a> issued for July 26, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> It climbed to number one on September 10, 2014, replacing Taylor Swift's "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shake_It_Off_(Taylor_Swift_song)" title="Shake It Off (Taylor Swift song)">Shake It Off</a>" at the top of the chart. The peak position made Trainor the 21st female artist in <i>Billboard</i> history to top the chart with their debut hit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Additionally, "All About That Bass" spent 25 consecutive weeks in the Billboard Hot 100's top 10, becoming the tenth song to achieve this milestone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> On September 20, 2014, it became the fourth song in <i>Billboard</i> history to simultaneously attain top gainer awards on Digital Songs, Radio Songs and Streaming Songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_History_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_History-80">[80]</a></sup> It was the Hot 100's top airplay gainer for five consecutive weeks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Record_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Record-81">[81]</a></sup> becoming the first debut Hot 100 entry by a woman to do so since <a href="/wiki/Ashanti_(singer)" title="Ashanti (singer)">Ashanti</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Foolish_(Ashanti_song)" title="Foolish (Ashanti song)">Foolish</a>" (2002).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gary_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gary-82">[82]</a></sup> "All About That Bass" became the longest-running <i>Billboard</i> Hot 100 number one by an Epic Records artist after it spent eight consecutive weeks atop the chart. After its eighth week atop the chart, it was finally succeeded by Taylor Swift's "Shake It Off", which had spent eight weeks at number two. As "All About That Bass" and "Shake It Off" occupied the top two of the Hot 100 chart for eleven consecutive weeks, it marks the longest span for the top two positions being held by songs by female artists consecutively since <a href="/wiki/Alicia_Keys" title="Alicia Keys">Alicia Keys</a>' "<a href="/wiki/Fallin%27_(Alicia_Keys_song)" title="Fallin' (Alicia Keys song)">Fallin'</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Lopez" title="Jennifer Lopez">Jennifer Lopez</a>'s "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I%27m_Real_(Murder_Remix)" title="I'm Real (Murder Remix)">I'm Real (Murder Remix)</a>" locked the top two for ten weeks in 2001. "All About That Bass" marked the longest-running <i>Billboard</i> Hot 100 number one by a female artist in 2014, and the longest since <a href="/wiki/Lorde" title="Lorde">Lorde</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Royals_(song)" title="Royals (song)">Royals</a>" (2013).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>"All About That Bass" led an all-female top five on the Hot 100 for six consecutive weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> It is one of six singles, and the sole debut single to spend 15 weeks in the top two in the history of the <i>Billboard</i> Hot 100.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_History_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_History-80">[80]</a></sup> "All About That Bass", together with Swift's "Shake It Off" and "<a href="/wiki/Blank_Space" title="Blank Space">Blank Space</a>", occupied the Hot 100's top two for 14 consecutive weeks, marking the longest top two reign by any two acts in the chart's history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Record_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Record-81">[81]</a></sup> "All About That Bass" ranked at number eight on <i>Billboard</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s Hot 100 year-end chart for 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hot_100_YE_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hot_100_YE-84">[84]</a></sup> and was certified six-times platinum by the <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">Recording Industry Association of America</a> (RIAA) on January 7, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riaa_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-riaa-85">[85]</a></sup> The song sold 1.8 million copies during its reign at number one, and it became the fourth best-selling song of 2014 in the United States with 4.38 million copies sold for the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us_sales_2014_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-us_sales_2014-86">[86]</a></sup> As of February 2015, the song has accumulated a total of 4.6 million units sold in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nielsen_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-nielsen-87">[87]</a></sup>As of July 7th, 2016, the single has sold 5.55 million copies in United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> In Canada, "All About That Bass" debuted at number 82 on the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Hot_100" title="Canadian Hot 100">Canadian Hot 100</a> for the issue dated July 26, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The song peaked at number one on the issue dated September 20, 2014, before being replaced by Swift's "Shake It Off" the following week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> However, the track replaced "Shake It Off" the week after and went on to spend a total of eight non-consecutive weeks at number one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> It was certified quadruple platinum by <a href="/wiki/Music_Canada" title="Music Canada">Music Canada</a> for sales of 320,000 copies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canada-92">[92]</a></sup> was the seventh most successful single on the Canadian Hot 100 for 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada_YE_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canada_YE-93">[93]</a></sup> and has sold 408,000 copies as of January 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CANsales_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-CANsales-94">[94]</a></sup> In Mexico, the song peaked at number one for three consecutive weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mexico_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mexico-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania_and_Europe">Oceania and Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Oceania and Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>"All About That Bass" debuted at number 36 on the <a href="/wiki/ARIA_Charts" title="ARIA Charts">Australian Singles Chart</a> on August 3, 2014. The song reached a peak of number one on August 17, 2014, spent a total of four non-consecutive weeks atop the chart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AUS_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-AUS-96">[96]</a></sup> It was certified septuple platinum by the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Recording_Industry_Association" title="Australian Recording Industry Association">Australian Recording Industry Association</a> (ARIA), indicating sales of 490,000 copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARIA_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-ARIA-97">[97]</a></sup> The song was the second best-selling single of 2014 in Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AUS_ye_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-AUS_ye-98">[98]</a></sup> "All About That Bass" entered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Singles_Chart" title="New Zealand Singles Chart">New Zealand Singles Chart</a> at number 14 on August 4, 2014. It climbed to number one two weeks later and went on to accumulate six non-consecutive weeks at the chart's summit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NZ_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-NZ-99">[99]</a></sup> The single received a triple platinum certification from <a href="/wiki/Recorded_Music_NZ" title="Recorded Music NZ">Recorded Music NZ</a> (RMNZ), denoting sales of 45,000 units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RIANZ_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-RIANZ-100">[100]</a></sup> It became the second best-selling single of 2014 in New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NZ_ye_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-NZ_ye-101">[101]</a></sup> "All About That Bass" debuted at number 75 on the <a href="/wiki/UK_Singles_Chart" title="UK Singles Chart">UK Singles Chart</a>. Its sales of 4,349 units that week were drawn solely from streaming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Spotify substantially contributed to the song's success in the country. During its first six weeks of availability on streaming services in the United Kingdom, "All About That Bass" generated 1.18 million streams. 90% of these streams were through Spotify, which sent the song to number 33 on the UK Singles Chart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Analysis_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Analysis-45">[45]</a></sup> It made British chart history by becoming the first single to enter the chart's top 40 based on streaming alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the digital release of "All About That Bass" the week after, it climbed to number one on the UK Singles Chart, with sales in excess of 144,000 units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> It became the second-fastest selling single of the year in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-one_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-one-105">[105]</a></sup> "All About That Bass" returned six-figure sales in the UK for three consecutive weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-One_Direction_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-One_Direction-106">[106]</a></sup> The song was the longest-running number one of 2014 in the United Kingdom, alongside "<a href="/wiki/Happy_(Pharrell_Williams_song)" title="Happy (Pharrell Williams song)">Happy</a>" and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rather_Be_(Clean_Bandit_song)" title="Rather Be (Clean Bandit song)">Rather Be</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> On November 14, 2014, the song was certified platinum by the <a href="/wiki/British_Phonographic_Industry" title="British Phonographic Industry">British Phonographic Industry</a> (BPI).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BPI_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-BPI-108">[108]</a></sup> As of December 31, 2014, "All About That Bass" had sold a total of 651,000 units in the United Kingdom, and ended the year as the ninth best-selling in the country for 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UKsales_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-UKsales-109">[109]</a></sup> In Ireland, "All About That Bass" debuted at number 76 on the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Singles_Chart" title="Irish Singles Chart">Irish Singles Chart</a> issued for August 14, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> On October 2, 2014, the song climbed to the top of the chart,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> and went on to spend a total of four consecutive weeks at number one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Elsewhere in Europe, "All About That Bass" remained number one in Austria and Germany for six weeks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Austria_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Austria-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Germany_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Germany-114">[114]</a></sup> Denmark and Switzerland for five,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denmark_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Denmark-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Switzerland_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Switzerland-116">[116]</a></sup> Scotland for four,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scotland_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scotland-117">[117]</a></sup> Spain for three,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spain_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spain-118">[118]</a></sup> Poland for two,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poland_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poland-119">[119]</a></sup> and Hungary, Slovakia and Luxembourg for one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hungary_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hungary-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slovakia_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slovakia-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luxembourg_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luxembourg-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_video">Music video</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Music video">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background_and_concept">Background and concept</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Background and concept">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>"All About That Bass" was accompanied by a <a href="/wiki/Music_video" title="Music video">music video</a> directed by <a href="/wiki/Fatima_Robinson" title="Fatima Robinson">Fatima Robinson</a> and choreographed by Charm La'Donna. The clip was shot over a two-day period which began on May 8, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huffington_Post_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huffington_Post-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cape_Cod_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cape_Cod-124">[124]</a></sup> It was premiered online by music website <a href="/wiki/Idolator_(website)" title="Idolator (website)">Idolator</a> on June 10, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Idolator_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-Idolator-25">[25]</a></sup> Robinson conceptualized the clip's look "with pastel colors ", trying to show Trainor as "a little innocent-looking girl doing, like, booty-bumping dance moves", to which Trainor agreed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cosmo_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cosmo-8">[8]</a></sup> Robinson and Trainor wanted to make the visual as fun as possible to co-relate with the song's lyrical themes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cosmo_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cosmo-8">[8]</a></sup> When Trainor first received the video, she cried, and said, "I don't want to do this anymore".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rolling_Stone_Interview_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-Rolling_Stone_Interview-6">[6]</a></sup> As a result, Trainor was involved in performing several edits to the clip because she disapproved on some of its shots and felt her face looked "weird" in some segments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rolling_Stone_Interview_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-Rolling_Stone_Interview-6">[6]</a></sup> She later approved of the video's final edit and felt that it depicted her as a "pop star".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rolling_Stone_Interview_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-Rolling_Stone_Interview-6">[6]</a></sup> Trainor said she envisioned it as "a[n] [adorable] cartoon" she would play, but then the video became "bigger than anyone expected, it's like, crap, I have to kind of look like that now."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boston_Globe_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boston_Globe-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">Social media</a> played a key role in how the video was made and marketed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NY_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-NY-126">[126]</a></sup> Robinson discovered one of the video's dancers, Sione Maraschino, online through social media service <a href="/wiki/Vine_(service)" title="Vine (service)">Vine</a>, where Maraschino rose to popularity. Maraschino later shared the "All About That Bass" music video with his following on Twitter and <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a> which became a contributing factor to the clip going viral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NY_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-NY-126">[126]</a></sup> Trainor stated she could not dance and that Robinson "made [her] a rock star in two days".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cape_Cod_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cape_Cod-124">[124]</a></sup> She added, "Charm made up most of those dance moves, and taught me how to perform and 'make sure you smile!'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Becky_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Becky-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Synopsis">Synopsis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Synopsis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:289px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:All_About_That_Bass_screenshot.jpg"><img alt="Five women dancing in front of pastel-colored pink backdrop." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="166" data-file-width="287" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/51/All_About_That_Bass_screenshot.jpg" width="287"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:All_About_That_Bass_screenshot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Trainor and her accompanying dancers performing intricate choreography in front of a pink pastel-colored backdrop in the music video for "All About That Bass".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Predominantly, the music video comprises candy colors and choreography,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EW_Interview_2_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-EW_Interview_2-14">[14]</a></sup> and a pastel 1950s-theme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmanica_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carmanica-58">[58]</a></sup> It features Trainor in an uncharacteristic wardrobe of sweaters and white knee socks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boston_Globe_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boston_Globe-125">[125]</a></sup> It opens with Trainor dancing in front of a pink pastel backdrop. She sports a light blue collared sweater beneath a <a href="/wiki/Sundress" title="Sundress">sundress</a> of the same color, and a material bow <a href="/wiki/Headpiece" title="Headpiece">headpiece</a>. The clip then switches to a scene with Trainor wearing a headpiece made of roses and a light pink collared-sweater beneath a sundress of the same color. The video returns to the original scene where Trainor engages in a dance scene with four other female dancers who sport similar outfits to her. The scene later sees the five girls dancing while sitting on chairs. The routine is intercepted with Maraschino donning a white collared shirt and light blue shorts performing a dance sequence on his own, which includes him doing <a href="/wiki/Split_(gymnastics)" title="Split (gymnastics)">split acrobatics</a>. Three women, wearing platinum blonde wigs that cover their forehead and eyes, are also pictured in their own dance routine.</p>
<p>In another scene, Trainor, with her hair now loose, sports a yellow sweater and sings in front of a variety of colorful balloons and on top of a wide range of colored material. The singer is also pictured at a dining table topped with candy, offering an unhappy young man a cupcake and forcing him to smile with her hands. Two children, in a separate scene, are shown playing with dolls in a dollhouse, dancing in a bedroom and riding bicycles. Trainor is later seen posing next to the dollhouse, and while singing, "I won't be no stick-figure, silicone Barbie doll," tosses the doll across the room. In another segment, other girls are pictured smiling while holding magazines with their faces on the front covers.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The music video resulted in "All About That Bass" rising to prominence as a viral hit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EW_Interview_2_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-EW_Interview_2-14">[14]</a></sup> Jim Farber of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_News_(New_York)" title="Daily News (New York)">New York Daily News</a></i> observed "the 'debate' over [Trainor's] size—is she too big? Too small?—has taken on a life of its own" on the commentaries section of the video on <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jim_Farber_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jim_Farber-13">[13]</a></sup> It was YouTube's most-streamed music video for most of September and October 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slate_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-Slate-28">[28]</a></sup> and was the ninth most-viewed music video on the website for 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> It was <a href="/wiki/Vevo" title="Vevo">Vevo</a>'s second most streamed music video of the year, behind <a href="/wiki/Iggy_Azalea" title="Iggy Azalea">Iggy Azalea</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Fancy_(Iggy_Azalea_song)" title="Fancy (Iggy Azalea song)">Fancy</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> On August 24, 2015, the video reached 1 billion views on <a href="/wiki/Vevo" title="Vevo">Vevo</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> and is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_most_viewed_YouTube_videos" title="List of most viewed YouTube videos">fourteenth most viewed YouTube video of all time</a> with over 1.70 billion views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>The music video garnered generally favorable reviews from music critics. Yahoo! writer Paul Grein opined that the clip was the leading candidate to win Best Song with a Social Message at the 2015 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MTV_Video_Music_Awards" title="MTV Video Music Awards">MTV Video Music Awards</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yahoo.21_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yahoo.21-53">[53]</a></sup> <i>Cosmopolitan</i> editor Joanna Coles stated that her readers and staff saw Trainor as an ambassador for embracing creativity and individuality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NY_126-2"><a href="#cite_note-NY-126">[126]</a></sup> Evan Sawdey of <i>PopMatters</i> deemed the video "fun and buoyant".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopMatters_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-PopMatters-30">[30]</a></sup> Jon Carmanic of <i>The New York Times</i> opined that the video "bolstered" the song and called it "vivid".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carmanica_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Carmanica-58">[58]</a></sup> Idolator's Robbie Daw praised the clip's "memorable" dance routine and vintage aesthetic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Becky_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-Becky-61">[61]</a></sup> <i>Out</i> journalist Stacy Lambe described it as "a retro pop world that makes you want to dance in your seat".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Out_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-Out-24">[24]</a></sup> According to Erin Kean from <a href="/wiki/Salon_(website)" title="Salon (website)">Salon</a>, the clip offered a hard sell for the song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lauren Valenti of <i><a href="/wiki/Marie_Claire" title="Marie Claire">Marie Claire</a></i> felt that the video put an end to the unattainable standards of the beauty industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Beast" title="The Daily Beast">The Daily Beast</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s Marlow Stern called the visual "zesty,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beast_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-Beast-20">[20]</a></sup> while <i><a href="/wiki/USA_Today" title="USA Today">USA Today</a></i> writer Brian Mansfield described the clip as "'<a href="/wiki/Baby_Got_Back" title="Baby Got Back">Baby Got Back</a>'-meets-'<a href="/wiki/Beauty_School_Dropout" title="Beauty School Dropout">Beauty School Dropout</a>'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USA_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-USA-27">[27]</a></sup> Stereogum's Chris DeVille commented, "the video, for all its foibles, is both cute and effective".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stereogum_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-Stereogum-29">[29]</a></sup> <i>Billboard</i> writer Andrew Hampp described the clip as "slyly satirical".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Lips_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Lips-133">[133]</a></sup> Julie Zeilinger of the same publisher gave the clip a negative review, and wrote that Trainor was "mimicking music videos by the 'silicone Barbie dolls' her song references".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Julie_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Julie-134">[134]</a></sup> Zeilinger opined that the clip's body imagery should have imparted a different message and stated that Trainor "points out the focus on women's appearance... by focusing on women's appearance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Julie_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Julie-134">[134]</a></sup> Emma Garland of <i>Vice</i> opined that it was fun, safe and easily palatable. However, she felt that the clip did not require much thought and dismissed its choreography as the kind "year 2 schoolgirls would invent."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vice_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vice-39">[39]</a></sup> Diana Cook of <a href="/wiki/Cracked.com" title="Cracked.com"><i>Cracked</i></a> considered the video for the song an example of skinny-bashing and noted a double standard: "imagine if <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Swift" title="Taylor Swift">Taylor Swift</a> started singing about how boys prefer her awesome pert ass, while dancing around a chubby chick ... the Internet would explode."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fader" title="The Fader">The Fader</a></i> placed the music video third in their list, "Music's 8 Most Cringe-Worthy Acts Of Cultural Appropriation In 2014".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Live_performances">Live performances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Live performances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meghan_Trainor_(15378489253).jpg"><img alt="A young long-haired blonde woman singing into a microphone onstage. She sports a black skirt and black Bad Gal jacket. On her left and right are two brunette women dancing, each are wearing a sleeveless white top and leather shorts. A portraits of several bass speakers squared in pattern with background colors of neon green as the women's backdrop, as well as the iHeart Radio logo." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Meghan_Trainor_%2815378489253%29.jpg/220px-Meghan_Trainor_%2815378489253%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Meghan_Trainor_%2815378489253%29.jpg/330px-Meghan_Trainor_%2815378489253%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Meghan_Trainor_%2815378489253%29.jpg/440px-Meghan_Trainor_%2815378489253%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meghan_Trainor_(15378489253).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Trainor performing "All About That Bass" during the <a href="/wiki/Jingle_Ball_Tour_2014" title="Jingle Ball Tour 2014">Jingle Ball Tour</a> on December 10, 2014.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Trainor first performed "All About That Bass" live in Nashville at an <a href="/wiki/Emily_West" title="Emily West">Emily West</a> concert on July 16, 2014, and made her live television debut on <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Live!_with_Kelly_and_Michael" title="Live! with Kelly and Michael">Live! with Kelly and Michael</a></i> where she performed the song on August 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tenn_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-tenn-137">[137]</a></sup> On September 2, Trainor performed an acoustic version of "All About That Bass" for <i><a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Tonight" title="Entertainment Tonight">Entertainment Tonight</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> On September 4, Trainor performed "All About That Bass" on <i>The Tonight Show Starring Jimmy Fallon</i>. The rendition featured <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Fallon" title="Jimmy Fallon">Jimmy Fallon</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Roots" title="The Roots">The Roots</a>, who made use of a variety classroom instruments for the performance. <i>Rolling Stone</i> writer Ryan Reed commented, "Their unlikely interpretation was just as good as the original, with their sparse percussion and intimate doo-wop harmonies anchoring Trainor's passionate lines".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rolling_Stone_2_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rolling_Stone_2-76">[76]</a></sup> On September 11, Trainor performed "All About That Bass" on <i>The Ellen DeGeneres Show</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> She made her Australian television debut on September 15, performing the song on <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_X_Factor_(Australia_series_6)" title="The X Factor (Australia series 6)">The X Factor Australia</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> On September 18, <i>Billboard</i> published a mash-up of "All About That Bass" and Taylor Swift's "Shake It Off" which Trainor performed for Australian radio station <a href="/wiki/2Day_FM" title="2Day FM">2Day FM</a> that week. A journalist from the magazine, Erin Strecker, wrote, "The results aren't a huge surprise—mash up two super-catchy tracks and the result is, well, a catchy track".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Erin_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Erin-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>Trainor performed the track in a duet with Miranda Lambert at the Country Music Association Awards on November 5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CMT_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-CMT-63">[63]</a></sup> Lambert previously covered the song live in concert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Following the performance at the ceremony, American singer <a href="/wiki/Brad_Paisley" title="Brad Paisley">Brad Paisley</a> stepped into the audience and told Trainor that he felt she belonged in country music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> On November 26, Trainor performed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medley_(music)" title="Medley (music)">medley</a> of "All About That Bass" and "<a href="/wiki/Lips_Are_Movin" title="Lips Are Movin">Lips Are Movin</a>" on the finale of the <a href="/wiki/Dancing_with_the_Stars_(U.S._season_19)" title="Dancing with the Stars (U.S. season 19)">nineteenth season</a> of American series <i><a href="/wiki/Dancing_with_the_Stars_(U.S._TV_series)" title="Dancing with the Stars (U.S. TV series)">Dancing with the Stars</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> She also performed the track regularly as part of her set for the <a href="/wiki/Jingle_Ball_Tour_2014" title="Jingle Ball Tour 2014">Jingle Ball Tour 2014</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> On December 13, Trainor performed the song live on the finale of the <a href="/wiki/The_X_Factor_(UK_series_11)" title="The X Factor (UK series 11)">eleventh series of <i>The X Factor</i> UK</a>, with finalists <a href="/wiki/List_of_The_X_Factor_finalists_(UK_series_11)" title="List of The X Factor finalists (UK series 11)">Andrea Faustini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fleur_East" title="Fleur East">Fleur East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ben_Haenow" title="Ben Haenow">Ben Haenow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STV_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-STV-146">[146]</a></sup> "All About That Bass" will be featured on the new <a href="/wiki/Video_game" title="Video game">video game</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_video_game_franchises" title="List of video game franchises">series</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Just_Dance_2016" title="Just Dance 2016">Just Dance 2016</a></i>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_impact">Cultural impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cultural impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Josh Duboff of <i><a href="/wiki/Vanity_Fair_(magazine)" title="Vanity Fair (magazine)">Vanity Fair</a></i> wrote that the song had achieved "pop-cultural touchstone-status",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> while <i>The New York Times</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s Joe Coscarelli called the track "a cultural phenomenon".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-10">[10]</a></sup> Yahoo! published that the song was the eighth most-searched topic on their website for 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> According to Caroline Sullivan of <i>The Guardian</i> the song resulted in Trainor being viewed as "the poster girl for the larger woman" and "pop's emblem of self-acceptance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian_Interview_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_Interview-7">[7]</a></sup> <i>Billboard</i> wrote that the success of "All About That Bass" made Trainor a "breakthrough star virtually overnight" and one of the "biggest breakout stars" of 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Lips_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Lips-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Kadish_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Kadish-149">[149]</a></sup> In a different publication, the magazine wrote that the track became "a water cooler topic among young girls and parents".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Interview_3_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Interview_3-15">[15]</a></sup> In a press release, Trainor highlighted: "Some girls are like, 'I've hated myself. I hated life. I didn't want to go to school. I get bullied. And then I heard your song and I cried'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Interview_3_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Interview_3-15">[15]</a></sup> Following the song's rise to prominence, its co-writer and producer, Kevin Kadish, was signed to <a href="/wiki/Sony/ATV_Music_Publishing" title="Sony/ATV Music Publishing">Sony/ATV Music Publishing</a> in October 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Kadish_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Kadish-149">[149]</a></sup> Sony/ATV CEO, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Bandier" title="Martin Bandier">Martin Bandier</a>, stated, "'All About That Bass' is clearly one of the biggest songs of the year and we know there is a lot more to come from him".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> According to <i>Billboard</i> the song's success "also brought acclaim" to Trainor's label, Epic, by becoming the label's first number one single on the <i>Billboard</i> Hot 100 since <a href="/wiki/Sean_Kingston" title="Sean Kingston">Sean Kingston</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Beautiful_Girls_(Sean_Kingston_song)" title="Beautiful Girls (Sean Kingston song)">Beautiful Girls</a>" (2007).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gary_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gary-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;max-width:242px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:119px;max-width:119px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MinajHammersteinBallroomNYC.jpg"><img alt="A portrait of Nicki Minaj in a black outfit singing into a microphone." data-file-height="947" data-file-width="683" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/MinajHammersteinBallroomNYC.jpg/117px-MinajHammersteinBallroomNYC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/MinajHammersteinBallroomNYC.jpg/176px-MinajHammersteinBallroomNYC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/MinajHammersteinBallroomNYC.jpg/234px-MinajHammersteinBallroomNYC.jpg 2x" width="117"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:119px;max-width:119px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jennifer_Lopez_at_GLAAD_Media_Awards.jpg"><img alt="A portrait of Jennifer Lopez posing onstage in a black see-through outfit." data-file-height="1027" data-file-width="738" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jennifer_Lopez_at_GLAAD_Media_Awards.jpg/117px-Jennifer_Lopez_at_GLAAD_Media_Awards.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jennifer_Lopez_at_GLAAD_Media_Awards.jpg/176px-Jennifer_Lopez_at_GLAAD_Media_Awards.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jennifer_Lopez_at_GLAAD_Media_Awards.jpg/234px-Jennifer_Lopez_at_GLAAD_Media_Awards.jpg 2x" width="117"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">"All About That Bass" along with "<a href="/wiki/Anaconda_(Nicki_Minaj_song)" title="Anaconda (Nicki Minaj song)">Anaconda</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Nicki_Minaj" title="Nicki Minaj">Nicki Minaj</a> (<i>left</i>) and "<a href="/wiki/Booty_(song)" title="Booty (song)">Booty</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Lopez" title="Jennifer Lopez">Jennifer Lopez</a> (<i>right</i>) were recognized by a variety of publications for playing into pop culture's "Era of the Big Booty".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The success of "All About That Bass" played into <i><a href="/wiki/Vogue_(magazine)" title="Vogue (magazine)">Vogue</a></i> stating, "We're Officially in the Era of the Big Booty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Complex_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Complex-5">[5]</a></sup> On September 23, 2014, Steven J. Horowitz of <i>Billboard</i> wrote that "booty records" had "made a mainstream comeback thanks to records like Jennifer Lopez's 'Booty' and Meghan Trainor's 'All About That Bass'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Booty_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Booty-40">[40]</a></sup> Erika Ramirez of the same publication placed "All About That Bass" fifth in her list, Top 10 Moments of the Year in Ass, and deemed it 2014's "most catchy self-love song".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> The coinciding release of "All About That Bass" and Nicki Minaj's "Anaconda" in the United Kingdom resulted in a chart battle dubbed by British publications as "the battle of the booty songs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> <i>Rolling Stone</i> journalist Steve Knopper wrote, "If we assume the latest sing-about-your-butt trend in pop music has finally reached its, uh, conclusion, the unquestionable winner is Meghan Trainor's 'All About That Bass'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Sullivan opined that the three songs signaled a shift in mindset in pop history, where female artists frequently endured "scrutiny" from society for their weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sullivan_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sullivan-16">[16]</a></sup> Yomi Adegoke of <i>The Independent</i> wrote that the tracks helped "curves" reach the forefront of pop culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Independent_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Independent-73">[73]</a></sup> On November 11, 2014, it was reported that American company <a href="/wiki/Booty_Pop" title="Booty Pop">Booty Pop</a> experienced a 47% increase in demand as a result. A New York doctor, Matthew Schulman, told <i>Billboard</i> that Brazilian butt lift <a href="/wiki/Plastic_surgery" title="Plastic surgery">plastic surgery</a> had risen by 25% at his practice in 2014. Boston entrepreneur and gym instructor, Kelly Brabants stated in an interview with the magazine that she experienced a waiting list for her Booty by Brabants class. Brabants went on to opine, "It's not about being stick-thin anymore, every girl now wants a booty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parodies">Parodies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Parodies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The song's "smash hit status", according to Jason Lipshutz of <i>Billboard</i>, made it a popular focus of "viral videos and musical tributes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maejor_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Maejor-62">[62]</a></sup> On December 1, 2014, in an episode of Canadian comedy series <i><a href="/wiki/This_Hour_Has_22_Minutes" title="This Hour Has 22 Minutes">This Hour Has 22 Minutes</a></i>, the cast made a parody music video of the song, releasing it as a <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_of_Canada" title="Conservative Party of Canada">Conservative</a> political ad smearing the Federal <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_of_Canada" title="Liberal Party of Canada">Liberal Party</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Justin_Trudeau" title="Justin Trudeau">Justin Trudeau</a>, with the video titled "Just a Pretty Face."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> On December 7, <a href="/wiki/Nerdist_Industries" title="Nerdist Industries">Nerdist Industries</a> released a parody song "All About That Base: No Rebels", performed by <a href="/wiki/Team_Unicorn" title="Team Unicorn">Team Unicorn</a>. Their video comprised a <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars" title="Star Wars">Star Wars</a></i> theme and its premise sees men, depicted as <a href="/wiki/Stormtrooper_(Star_Wars)" title="Stormtrooper (Star Wars)">stormtroopers</a>, engaging in choreography alongside cheerleaders dressed in attire inspired by the fictional character <a href="/wiki/Darth_Vader" title="Darth Vader">Darth Vader</a>. Mitchell Peters of <i>Billboard</i> opined that the parody was "catchy" and "hilarious".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboard_Mitchell_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Billboard_Mitchell-157">[157]</a></sup> On December 12, a parody music video by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> entitled <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=kHy08ZPav88" rel="nofollow">"All About That Space"</a> went viral. The video featured NASA interns performing choreography and shots of the Johnston Space Center in <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a>. The parody also comprised altered lyrics written by Sarah Schlieder. NASA's parody video attained over one million views in four days. Lee Moran of the <i>New York Daily News</i> called the rendition "wonderfully a-dork-able".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> The parodies later led to <i>Time</i> publishing an article titled "No More 'All About That Bass' Parodies, Please" on December 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cover_versions">Cover versions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Cover versions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On August 4, 2014, American boy band <a href="/wiki/Emblem3" title="Emblem3">Emblem3</a> performed an acoustic cover of "All About That Bass".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> The Roots covered the song on <i>The Tonight Show Starring Jimmy Fallon</i> on August, 20. Their rendition was deemed "angelic" by Chris Payne of <i>Billboard</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Canadian singer <a href="/wiki/Justin_Bieber" title="Justin Bieber">Justin Bieber</a> posted a cover version of "All About That Bass" on his <a href="/wiki/SoundCloud" title="SoundCloud">SoundCloud</a> on October 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTV_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-MTV-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early September 2014, <a href="/wiki/Kate_Davis" title="Kate Davis">Kate Davis</a> performed the song and played <a href="/wiki/Double_bass" title="Double bass">double bass</a> under the leadership of pianist <a href="/wiki/Scott_Bradlee" title="Scott Bradlee">Scott Bradlee</a>; in three months their 1940s jazz-style version, called "All About that (Upright) Bass", had received 8 million views on Bradlee's Postmodern Jukebox YouTube channel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 24, American group <a href="/wiki/Pentatonix" title="Pentatonix">Pentatonix</a>'s Avi Kaplan released his cover version of the song online. Kaplan's rendition was positively reviewed by James Grebey of <i>Spin</i> who wrote, "Trainor's tune sounds very different a few octaves lower," and added, "We think it might just be an improvement".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> "All About That Bass" was also covered by Jamaican-American singer <a href="/wiki/Anita_Antoinette" title="Anita Antoinette">Anita Antoinette</a> during the seventh season of the American series <i>The Voice</i>, receiving praise by the show's judges <a href="/wiki/Pharrell_Williams" title="Pharrell Williams">Pharrell Williams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adam_Levine" title="Adam Levine">Adam Levine</a>. Ashley Lee of <i>Billboard</i> wrote that Antoinette provided the song with "a <a href="/wiki/Reggae" title="Reggae">reggae</a> twist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the beginning of 2016 Trainor and <a href="/wiki/James_Corden" title="James Corden">James Corden</a> performed a parody of the song on failed New Year's resolutions about losing weight on the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Late_Late_Show_with_James_Corden" title="The Late Late Show with James Corden">The Late Late Show with James Corden</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_appearances">Media appearances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Media appearances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The song is featured in the film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Intern_(2015_film)" title="The Intern (2015 film)">The Intern</a></i> and the television shows <i><a href="/wiki/Red_Band_Society" title="Red Band Society">Red Band Society</a>, <a href="/wiki/Selfie_(TV_series)" title="Selfie (TV series)">Selfie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black-ish" title="Black-ish">Black-ish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Younger_(TV_series)" title="Younger (TV series)">Younger</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Undateable" title="Undateable">Undateable</a>.</i></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Charts">Charts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Charts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div>
<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;">
<tr>
<td style="width:50%; text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weekly_charts">Weekly charts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=All_About_That_Bass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Weekly charts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable plainrowheaders sortable">
<tr>
<th scope="col">Chart (2014–15)</th>
<th scope="col">Peak<br/>
position</th>
